<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2050-7496/2013">J. Mater. Chem. A</a></span>, 2024, <strong>12</strong>, 3133-3141</div><h1 id="sect181"><span class="title_heading">Boosting the electrochemical performance and moisture stability of O3-type NaNi<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathodes using novel Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> coatings prepared <span class="italic">via</span> a polyvinylpyrrolidone-anchored complex coating process<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Minjun 
            Kim
          
        
      </span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Minsu 
            Choi
          
        
      </span><span class="bold"> and </span>
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Wonchang 
            Choi
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Wonchang&nbsp;Choi (orcid.org/0000-0002-8463-1517) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-8463-1517"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*
      <br><span class="italic">Department of Energy Engineering, Konkuk University, 120, Neungdong-ro, Gwangjin-gu, Seoul 05029, Republic of Korea. E-mail: <a href="mailto:wcchoi@konkuk.ac.kr">wcchoi@konkuk.ac.kr</a></span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      4th October 2023
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 4th December 2023</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 5th December 2023</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>O3-type layered oxides are considered highly promising cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries owing to their notable specific capacities, high theoretical energy densities, and low costs. However, the applications of O3-type layered oxides are limited owing to their structural degradation during chargedischarge cycles and poor moisture stability, which negatively affect their performance and durability. Notably, surface modifications of these materials play a crucial role in mitigating these challenges by providing a protective layer safeguarding the cathode material. In this study, Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (NMO) was successfully coated onto the surface of Na(Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>)O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (NFM) using a polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP)-assisted wet chemical method. Despite their low costs and good electrical conductivities, Mo-based coatings are yet to be explored as potential coating materials for O3-type layered oxide cathodes. Here, the main PVP chain facilitates the formation of a uniform NMO coating layer by effectively anchoring Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> ions onto the surface of the (Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small><span class="italic">x</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O ((NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small>) precursor. The presence of the NMO nanocoating layer on the NFM surface was confirmed <span class="italic">via</span> various characterization techniques. The NMO-coated NFM exhibited notable improvements in electrochemical performance compared to pristine NFM. In addition, the NMO-coated NFM exhibited superior moisture stability. This study presents an effective strategy to simultaneously improve the electrochemical performance and moisture stability of O3-type layered cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect247"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Since the first proposition of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the 1970s, they have been widely applied in various fields, including electric vehicles and energy-storage systems, as the most important energy-storage devices. However, owing to the limited reserves and uneven distribution of lithium, the increased LIB production over the past 10 years has placed a significant burden on lithium supplies.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1,2</span></sup></a> Therefore, the development of LIBs is challenging, and this has necessitated further innovation and exploration of new systems. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are promising alternatives to LIBs as sodium is more abundant and inexpensive compared to lithium.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> SIBs can utilize large amounts of iron and manganese as alternatives to cobalt in the cathode materials, rendering them more sustainable and cost-effective options.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6,7</span></sup></a> Despite the inherent differences between LIBs and SIBs, the similar physical and chemical properties of lithium and sodium ions provide valuable insights into the properties of the main components of SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8</span></sup></a> Generally, because the cathode material is primarily responsible for major battery performance aspects, such as energy density, cycling performance, and cost, exploring high-performance cathode materials for SIBs is essential. Sodium-layered oxide materials are common alternatives to lithium-layered oxide materials. Therefore, several researchers have studied O3-type sodium-layered oxide materials owing to various advantages, including the intercalation ability of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> into the layered structure, a small degree of cation mixing, and a simple synthesis process.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">911</span></sup></a> Among the various sodium-layered oxide materials reported, O3-type layered Na(Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>)O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (NFM) is one of the most notable cathode materials for SIBs since it was first reported by the Johnson group.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> NFM exhibits an excellent capacity of approximately 130 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> in the voltage range of 2.04.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>) and structurally high reversibility during the desodiation/sodiation process.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9,13</span></sup></a> Notably, the Fe<small><sup>3+/4+</sup></small> redox system exhibits high activity in the O3-type NaFeO<small><sub>2</sub></small> system for SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> In contrast to LIBs, the high redox potential of Fe, as a transition metal in sodium-layered oxides, is less problematic in SIBs owing to their lower operating voltage range.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,16</span></sup></a> NFM cathode materials utilizing Fe instead of Co are particularly interesting in terms of their cost and environmental sustainability. Furthermore, it's important to note that Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small> is a Jahn-Teller active element. Excessive amounts of Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small> can lead to rapid capacity fading due to the Jahn-Teller distortion of Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small> within the layered structure of cathode materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17,18</span></sup></a> To suppress this phenomenon, the strategical incorporation of low-valent active transition metal ions (such as Ni and Fe) can be utilized in order to increase of oxidation state of Mn element and mitigate the Jahn-Teller behavior exhibited by Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small> in the active species.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">However, despite these advantages, exploring the degradation mechanism of NFM cathode materials and introducing further improvement strategies to achieve prolonged cycle life with high capacity are necessary. According to recent studies, the degradation of NFM can be attributed to its high surface reactivity.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7,20</span></sup></a> NFM may induce side reactions by establishing direct contact with the electrolyte, thereby forming a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21,22</span></sup></a> The occurrence of these severe side reactions and the formation of SEI layers can reduce both the capacity and cycle stability.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a> In addition, because this cathode material is air- and moisture-sensitive owing to its high surface reactivity, compounds such as Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> and NaOH are formed on the cathode material surface when exposed to humid air.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23,24</span></sup></a> This may, in turn, form a passivating layer on the cathode material surface and increase the internal resistance and impedance of the cell. In addition, commercialization is limited owing to difficulties in the processing, storage, and transportation of these materials and electrodes. Therefore, implementing surface modification strategies can be highly effective in alleviating the degradation caused by the surface reactivity to electrolytes, moisture, and air, effectively improving the electrochemical performance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Although successful surface coating studies have been reported for various cathode materials in both LIBs and SIBs, an efficient coating process and appropriate coating material, specifically for NFM cathode materials, are yet to be identified. Metal oxides, such as TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, ZrO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, and Al<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>, have been applied as coating materials through conventional solid-state methods to overcome the shortcomings in stability and electrochemical performance while retaining the various advantages of O3-type cathode materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20,2628</span></sup></a> Although these attempts demonstrate significant improvements in electrochemical performance and stability compared to the use of pristine NFM, the insulating metal-oxide coating layers formed by the conventional solid-state method are not continuous on the cathode material surface and block the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion conduction path. Ideally, the coating layer should not only completely cover the cathode material but also have high Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion conductivity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29</span></sup></a> Therefore, the optimal coating process and materials for O3-type cathode materials should be explored further.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Herein, we propose the Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (NMO) surface coating of NFM cathode material through the properties of polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) anchoring the metal-based cathode material surface and forming a complex with metal ions. Considering the properties of PVP that can easily form complex bonds with metal ions, we believe that PVP is anchored to the surface of the (Ni<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small><span class="italic">x</span>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O ((NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small>) precursor, and PVP subsequently forms successful complex with Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> ions on the surface of precursor material.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> Then, the active material precursor and coating material precursor undergo a simultaneous sodiation and oxidation process during main calcination with Na source. The PVP-anchored complex coating process allow for the even distribution of the coating material precursor before main calcination step, effectively preventing particle aggregation even when subjected to high-temperature sintering conditions. This approach is expected to maximize the electrochemical performance and moisture stability improvement effect as the coating layer completely covers the cathode active material even with a relatively small coating amount. In addition, NMO, newly introduced as a coating material in this study, offers several specific advantages compared with previously reported coating materials. In particular, NMO has good electrical conductivity, which facilitates electron transport within the cathode material, and NMO may contribute to Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion conductivity than conventional metal-oxide coating materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3133</span></sup></a> Also, NMO can be effective as a coating material for cathode materials because it is physically and chemically stable. In summary, we utilized a PVP-anchored complex coating process to uniformly deposit NMO coating layer onto the surface of an NFM cathode material.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect308"><span class="a_heading">Experimental</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect310"><span class="b_heading">Material synthesis</span></h3>
        <span>The (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursor was synthesized using the oxalate coprecipitation method. For the preparation of the (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursor powder, nickel(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) sulfate hexahydrate (NiSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>6H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O), iron(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) sulfate heptahydrate (FeSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>7H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O), and manganese(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) sulfate monohydrate (MnSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) were used as starting materials. First, an aqueous solution containing the required stoichiometric amounts of NiSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>6H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, FeSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>7H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, and MnSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O with a concentration of 0.5 M was slowly added to a 0.5 M ammonium oxalate ((NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small>) solution. The ratio of ammonium oxalate to transition metals was equimolar. Simultaneously, an appropriate amount of a 0.5 M ammonium hydroxide (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>OH) solution was added to the above solution to maintain the pH at 7. Throughout the coprecipitation process, the solution was stirred continuously for 3 h, and the temperature was maintained at 50 C. The resultant powder was filtered, washed with deionized water, and dried overnight at 100 C in a vacuum oven.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">The NMO coating on the NFM surface was prepared using a PVP-anchored complex coating process. The as-prepared (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursor (4.0 g) was added to a mixture of anhydrous ethanol and distilled water (1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1, vol%, 40 mL) and vigorously stirred until the powder was well dispersed. For coating with 1 wt% or 2 wt% NMO, 0.08 g or 0.16 g PVP (MW = 55<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">000) was dissolved in the above solution under constant magnetic stirring for 1 h. Subsequently, 0.0257 g or 0.0514 g of ammonium molybdate tetrahydrate ((NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>6</sub></small>Mo<small><sub>7</sub></small>O<small><sub>24</sub></small>4H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O), corresponding to 1 wt% or 2 wt% coatings, was added to the above solution, and the mixture was stirred at 85 C until the solvent was completely evaporated. The obtained powder was thoroughly mixed with sodium carbonate (Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>) and calcined at 860 C in air for 12 h to remove PVP and obtain NMO-coated materials. The total sodium content for each sample was calculated by summing the sodium contents required to form both the NFM parent material and NMO coating layer. To consider the loss of sodium during the high-temperature calcination process, an additional 5 wt% excess of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> was introduced, based on the calculated sodium content requirements mentioned above. For the uncoated NFM, the PVP-assisted coating process was skipped, and calcination was performed by mixing the (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursor with Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>. The same calcination conditions were used for the uncoated (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursors. The same calcination conditions were used for the uncoated (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursors. The uncoated NFM and 0.5 wt%, 1 wt%, 2 wt% and 30 wt% NMO-coated samples are denoted as pristine NFM and 0.5 wt%, 1 wt%, 2 wt% and 30 wt% NMONFM, respectively.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect356"><span class="b_heading">Materials characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The crystalline phases of the pristine NFM and NMONFM were analyzed using powder X-ray diffraction (XRD; SmartLab, Rigaku) with a Cu K radiation source in the 2<span class="italic"></span> range of 1080 at a scanning rate of 5 min<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The elemental compositions and valences were determined by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS; K-Alpha, Thermo Scientific), and all spectra were corrected considering the C 1s peak as the standard peak. The morphologies of the particles were examined using field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM; SU8010, Hitachi High-Tech). The crystal structures and coating-layer thicknesses of the samples were observed using high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (TEM; JEM-ARM 200F, JEOL), and elemental distributions were acquired using energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). Inductively coupled plasma-mass spectrometry (ICP-MS; Agilent 7700X, Agilent Technologies) was conducted for the powder samples to investigate the elemental compositions of cathode materials. The XPS analysis of the material exposed to water was performed by exposing 1 g of the material to 1 mL of water and immediately drying it at 100 C for 3 h in a vacuum oven.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect361"><span class="b_heading">Cell assembly and electrochemical testing</span></h3>
        <span>The electrochemical behavior of the pristine NFM and NMONFM cathode materials was analyzed using CR-2032-type coin cells. The active materials (pristine NFM or NMONFM powder), Super P (carbon black), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVdF) were weighed at a mass ratio of 8<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1, and an appropriate amount of <span class="italic">N</span>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone was added to control the viscosity. Simultaneously, the solution was mixed until the formation of a uniform slurry. The obtained slurry was cast onto an Al foil, followed by drying at 80 C for 12 h under vacuum and punching into a round disk with a diameter of 12 mm. The prepared cathodes were selected as the working electrodes, and an Na metal disk with a diameter of 15 mm was selected as the counter electrode. A glass fiber filter with a diameter of 18 mm was used as the separator. The electrolyte solution comprised 1.0 M sodium perchlorate (NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small>) in a mixed solvent of ethylene carbonate (EC), propylene carbonate (PC), and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1, vol%) with 3 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). All the coin-cell assembly processes were carried out in an argon-filled glove box. The cell performance, in terms of galvanostatic chargingdischarging, cyclic voltammetry (CV), cyclability, and rate capability, was examined using the WBCS 3000 (WonATech) battery cycler in the voltage range of 2.04.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>) at room temperature (25 C). The current densities and specific capacities of the samples were calculated from the masses of the active materials.</span>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect368"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a> illustrates the mechanism of the PVP-anchored complex coating process for uniformly coating NMO onto the NFM cathode material surface. Previous studies have reported the use of PVP, as a connecting medium between the cathode active materials for LIBs and SIBs and the coating layer, to obtain uniform coatings.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,3437</span></sup></a> In this study, first, PVP could easily anchor to (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> owing to its ability to donate a pair of electrons from the carbonyl oxygen to the metal cations on the surface or form complex bonds between nitrogen in five-membered nitrogen-containing heterocycles and metal cations.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34,35</span></sup></a> Subsequently, after the addition of (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>6</sub></small>Mo<small><sub>7</sub></small>O<small><sub>24</sub></small>4H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, the Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> ions complexed with PVP.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> Finally, the (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> coated with Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> ion-complexed PVP underwent synchronous sodiation. Through the calcination process at 860 C in air with the Na source, the Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> ion-complexed PVP on the surface underwent conversion to NMO, whereas the (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursor at the center was transformed into NFM during the calcination process with Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> at 860 C in air for 12 h. In addition, PVP on the (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursor surface was completely removed during heat treatment in air.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,34,37</span></sup></a> Typically, achieving a uniformly coated layer on the surface of a cathode material using conventional methods is often challenging because the coating precursor tends to exhibit an uneven distribution during the calcination process.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> Using the PVP-anchored complex coating process, the well-coated PVP on the (NFM)C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursor surface served as an effective connecting medium, facilitating the formation of a uniform NMO nanocoating layer. Furthermore, sodiation and oxidation of the active material precursor and coating material precursors were carried out simultaneously in a single step, making this method both simple and effective for coating.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10,39</span></sup></a></span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ta06034a-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic of the PVP-anchored complex coating process.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The crystal structures of NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM were characterized by XRD, and the results are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>. The results indicate that the patterns of all the samples can be indexed to the O3-type -NaFeO<small><sub>2</sub></small> structure (<span class="italic">R</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> space group).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> All diffraction peaks were sharp and had high intensities, indicating that all samples were well crystallized. No peak shift was found in any of the NMO-coated samples, indicating that the coating process, the PVP-anchored complex coating process, did not affect the bulk crystal structure of the NFM. Meanwhile, no characteristic peaks were observed, proving the existence of the NMO coating layer, in the XRD patterns of 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM. This can be attributed to the small amount of NMO. Since the crystallinity of the NFM cathode material is very strong, the peaks of coating materials with low contents are likely to be buried in noise on the XRD pattern. To verify that the NMO was well coated using the PVP-anchored complex coating process, the amount of NMO was increased to 30 wt%. In the XRD pattern of 30 wt% NMONFM, evident diffraction peaks that matched well with those of PDF #00-012-0773 were observed.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> Therefore, the PVP-anchored complex coating process successfully coated NMO onto NFM particles.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ta06034a-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) XRD patterns of the pristine NFM and all NMO-coated samples. XPS spectra of (b) Ni 2p, (c) Fe 2p, (d) Mn 2p, and (e) Mo 3d for the pristine NFM and all NMO-coated samples.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To investigate the influence of the NMO coating on the chemical valence state of the NFM cathode material, an XPS analysis was conducted. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2bd</a> present the XPS spectra of Ni 2p, Fe 2p, and Mn 2p for pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM. In all the three samples, the Ni 2p spectrum (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>) displayed a spinorbit doublet, which was identified by the binding energies of 854.3 and 871.6 eV for the Ni 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and Ni 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> core levels, respectively. In addition, the spectra displayed significant shake-up satellites at 860.4 and 878.7 eV. Because unresolved multiplet splitting causes slight asymmetry in the high-binding-energy side of the principal photoemission lines, the fit included two supplementary components (MS comp.) at 856.8 and 874.7 eV.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> The Fe 2p spectra (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a>) for all samples were fitted with two main peaks and two small shake-up satellites: two main Fe 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and Fe 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks located at 710.5 and 723.9 eV and two shake-up satellites located at 731.1 and 717.7 eV, respectively. The Mn 2p spectra (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a>) showed a broad spinorbit doublet: Mn 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and Mn 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks located at 641.96 and 653.2 eV, respectively. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2bd</a>, no significant differences were observed between the spectra of any of the samples. Consequently, the chemical valence state of the host-layered oxide remained unaffected by NMO modification. Furthermore, as shown in the Mo 3d spectra of 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM, the XPS analysis confirmed the presence of an NMO coating layer with Mo in the stabilized state of Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a>), which was evident from the characteristic binding energies of Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> 3d<small><sub>5/2</sub></small> (232.2 eV) and Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> 3d<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> (235.5 eV).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> However, the XPS spectrum of pristine NFM did not present any peaks at the binding energy positions corresponding to Mo 3d. This indicated that Mo was successfully distributed on the surfaces of 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM. The host-layered oxide remained unaffected by the NMO modification.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The morphological and structural features of pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM were characterized using FE-SEM (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3ac</a>). The SEM images presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3ac</a> indicate that the flake-shaped primary particles, with a size of approximately 1 m, aggregated to form secondary particles with an average size of 15 m in all the three samples. This type of morphology is a characteristic feature of several typical layered transition metal oxides.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> Therefore, the NFM cathode material was successfully synthesized through the oxalate coprecipitation method, and the NMO coating had no obvious effect on the particle size or shape.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ta06034a-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">FE-SEM images of (a) pristine NFM, (b) 1 wt% NMONFM, and (c) 2 wt% NMONFM. High-resolution (HR)-TEM images and lattice fringes of (d) 1 wt% NMONFM and (e) 2 wt% NMONFM. Line-scanning intensity profiles obtained from the area highlighted in (d) and (e).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d and e</a> show the HR-TEM images of 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM, respectively. The images reveal that the 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM samples had uniform coating layers on the NFM particle surfaces, with thicknesses of approximately 8 and 14 nm, respectively. Notably, the coating-layer thickness increased proportionally with the amount of coating material used. Clear lattice fringes were observed in the coating layer with an interplanar spacing of 0.275 nm, corresponding to the (311) plane of NMO.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> Also, as shown in Fig. S1,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> an interplanar distance of 0.322 nm was observed in the coating layer region of the 2 wt% NMONFM sample, which corresponds to the crystal plane (220).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a> The lattice fringes observed in HR-TEM images also correspond well to XRD characterization of NMO, clearly affirming the presence of the NMO coating layer on the NFM particles. These results demonstrate the successful surface coating of the NFM cathode materials using the PVP-anchored complex coating process.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Fig. S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> displays elemental mapping images obtained through EDS for 1 wt% NMONFM, illustrating the distribution of the main chemical elements within the NMO-coated NFM particles. As shown in Fig. S2,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the uniform distribution of Mo element of NMO was confirmed. Furthermore, the distributions of Mo exhibited overlap with the distributions of Ni, Fe, and Mn, the main elements of the cathode material. This observation serves as evidence that the NMO coating layer was uniformly distributed on the surface of the NFM cathode material.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Table S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> presents the results of the ICP analysis of elements in the final products, from which it can be seen that the molar ratio of Ni<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">Fe<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">Mn of all samples is close to 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1. The calculation of the NMO content was based on the assumption that all the molybdenum ions transform into NMO coating layer during the calcination process. For the nominal compositions of 1 and 2 wt% NMONFM materials, it was determined that the NMO content amounted to 0.95 and 1.98 wt%, respectively. The presence of the Mo element in the coated samples was successfully confirmed through ICP-MS analysis, supporting the effective formation of the coating layer <span class="italic">via</span> the PVP-anchored complex coating process.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The electrochemical performance of the as-synthesized samples was tested using coin-type half cells in the voltage range of 2.04.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a> shows the initial charge/discharge profiles of the pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM at a current density of 0.1C (1C = 130 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). The initial charge/discharge capacities were 136.68/129.48, 139.22/127.96, and 137.58/126.87 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM, respectively. The 1<small><sup>st</sup></small> cycle discharge capacities decreased with increasing amounts of coating material, which can be attributed to the reduction in the content of the cathode material caused by the addition of the coating material. The specific capacity was determined by the electrochemical reaction of the cathode material; because NMO was not involved in this reaction, its presence in the coating layer did not contribute to the specific capacity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ta06034a-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Initial chargedischarge curves of the pristine NFM and all the NMO-coated samples at a current density of 0.1C in the range of 2.04.0 V. (b) CV curves of the pristine NFM and all the NMO-coated samples at 0.1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a> displays the CV curves of the pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM electrodes in the voltage range of 2.04.0 V at 0.1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Generally, during the charge/discharge process, several pairs of redox potentials related to cathodic and anodic reactions are observed in the CV curves. The large peaks observed at approximately 3.0/2.8 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> correspond to the Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Ni<small><sup>4+</sup></small> redox couple, and the broad peaks observed around 3.6/3.5 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> are associated with the Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small>/Fe<small><sup>4+</sup></small> redox reaction, as reported in previous studies.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9,46</span></sup></a> The reversibility of the crystalline structural changes during repeated charge/discharge processes is strongly related to the structural stability and electrochemical performance of the NFM cathode material, which can be estimated from the intensity and position of the cathodic and anodic peaks in the CV curves.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9</span></sup></a> As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>, the anodic peak near 3.0 V of 1 wt% NMONFM showed strong intensity at relatively low potential compared to those of 2 wt% NMONFM and pristine NFM. This right shift is caused by an increase in overall overpotential in the charge process, which may affect the reversibility or structural stability of the cathode material. Therefore, among the tested samples, the 1 wt% NMONFM showed a peak at a low voltage, and a small overpotential occurred, as evidenced by the CV curves obtained during charge/discharge processes, and it will be discussed in more detail in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a>. Also, higher peak current corresponds to a more rapid and reversible redox reaction. The reduction peak intensity at about 2.8 V in the CV curve is relatively lower in the 2 wt% NMONFM sample, as compared to that in the 1 wt% NMONFM sample. This phenomenon indicates that the rate of intercalation/deintercalation reaction on the surface of the cathode material was slowed due to the excessive coating amount of 2 wt% NMONFM in spite of the electronically conductive NMO.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> In addition, the highly reversible CV curve implies its promising cycling stability.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ta06034a-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Cycling performance of the pristine NFM and all the NMO-coated samples at 1C. (b) Comparison of the average charge/discharge potentials as a function of the cycle number during long cycle test.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The cycling performance of the three samples was evaluated by testing them in the voltage range of 2.04.0 V at a current density of 1C to assess the effects of the coating content. The cell was initially cycled at a current rate of 0.1C for three cycles to undergo formation and subsequently cycled at a current rate of 1C. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a>, the discharge capacity retention after 100 cycles for pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM were 51.96%, 62.95%, and 57.45%, respectively. Both NMO-coated samples exhibited increased capacity retention over cycling, and 1 wt% NMONFM demonstrated the best cycling performance, which was significantly better than that of the pristine NFM. Also, <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a> demonstrated the coulombic efficiencies (CEs) of pristine NFM for the 1 wt% NMONFM and 2 wt% NMONFM during long-term cycling. All the electrodes showed a CE of around 91% at first cycle, indicating the similar values as reported in previous literature.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10,11</span></sup></a> Especially, the mean CE of 1 wt% NMONFM at 1C was greater than 99.2% during long-term cycling although the pristine electrode exhibited the mean CE around 98.7%. These results suggested that a coating content of 1 wt% NMO was optimal for improving the electrochemical performance of NFM cathode materials. An appropriate NMO coating significantly enhanced the cycling stability, as demonstrated by the improved capacity retention of the NMO-coated samples compared to that of pristine NFM. The improved cycling performance of the 1 wt% NMONFM was attributed to the protective NMO coating layer, which prevented direct contact between the NFM particles and electrolyte, thus inhibiting electrolyte decomposition and the growth of an undesirable SEI layer on the active material surface.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7,49</span></sup></a> In addition, the rapid decay of the discharge capacity during cycling was attributed to the surface structural deterioration of the NFM cathode material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23,50</span></sup></a> However, the NMO coating improved the surface structural stability of the NFM particles, contributing to the enhanced cycling stability of the NMO-coated samples.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a> depicts the average charge/discharge voltage variation with cycling for pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM. The increase in the average voltage is caused by an increase in the ionicity of the transition metaloxygen (TMO) bond in O3-type NaTMO<small><sub>2</sub></small> (TM = Ni, Fe, or Mn). During the charge and discharge cycles, the structure of the O3-type NaTMO<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode material may gradually collapse or undergo phase transitions due to the insertion and extraction of sodium ions.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51,52</span></sup></a> This can affect the ionicity of the TMO bond and the overall electrochemical performance of the material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3,5355</span></sup></a> After 100 cycles at 0.1C, the average discharge voltage of pristine NFM decreased significantly from 3.04 V to 2.71 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> (<span class="italic">V</span> = 0.33 V). By contrast, the average discharge voltage of 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM decreased slightly from 3.04 V to 2.91 V (<span class="italic">V</span> = 0.13 V) and from 3.06 V to 2.79 V (<span class="italic">V</span> = 0.27 V), respectively. The results indicated that the NMO-coated samples had improved structural stability and experienced a significantly lower increase in polarization during cycling than the pristine sample.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56</span></sup></a> Therefore, the NMO coating layer, which was distributed between the active material and electrolyte, effectively suppressed the rapid voltage decay of the O3-type layered oxide during cycling.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">To obtain more insight into the changes in the crystal structure of NFM during the electrochemical cycle, XRD analysis of cycled electrodes was conducted after 100 cycles at discharged state (Fig. S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). In the case of 1 wt% NMONFM, the original O3 phase maintained the major phase even after cycling. On the other hand, pristine NFM showed an overall low intensity of the main phase, and the appearance and presence of P3 phase was observed.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13,5760</span></sup></a> These results suggest that the capacity fade, associated with the loss of Na ions, is due to the structural instability of O3 type cathode material after long-term cycling. In addition, it implies and validates the remarkable structural reversibility and stability of the NFM cathode material especially near surface region owing to the NMO coating.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit61"><sup><span class="sup_ref">61</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">The rate capacities of pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM under different current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10C in the voltage range of 2.04.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>) are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>. These results demonstrated that the NMO-coated NFM cathode exhibited superior rate performance, particularly at a high current density. For example, at a current density of 1C, the discharge capacities of pristine NFM and 1 wt% and 2 wt% NMONFM were 97.05, 104.46, and 108.64 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. In addition, at a high current density of 10C, the discharge capacity of 1 wt% NMONFM remained as high as 81.13 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, whereas pristine NFM and 2 wt% NMONFM exhibited lower capacities of only 64.43 and 74.59 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. The improved electrochemical performance of the NMO-coated NFM can be attributed to enhanced Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion dynamics, as NMO is expected to have higher Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion conductivity than insulating metal-oxide coating materials.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit62"><sup><span class="sup_ref">62</span></sup></a> In addition, NMO exhibits good electrical conductivity, which facilitates electron transport within the cathode material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31,32</span></sup></a> Thus, the NMO coating layer significantly improved the electrochemical performance of the NFM cathode materials. Comparison of the rate performance of 1 wt% NMONFM and other SIBs cathode materials are listed in Table S2.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a><a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26,27,49,63,64</span></sup></a></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ta06034a-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">Rate capabilities of the pristine NFM and all the NMO-coated samples.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">In the process of forming a coating layer on the cathode material, if the coating layer does not completely cover the cathode material particles, the SEI layer becomes excessively thick due to side reactions during the cycle, and it results in degradation of electrochemical performance. However, an excessively thick coating layer to completely cover the cathode material particles may reduce the deintercalation/intercalation efficiency on the surface of the cathode material particles. Thus, optimizing the thickness of the coating layer is a very important step for the best rate performance. We performed a rate capability test for 0.5 wt% NMONFM to confirm the optimal coating thickness range. As shown in Fig. S4,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> 0.5 wt% NMONFM exhibited lower capacity of 67.90 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at a high current density of 10C. Because of its low coating content, the 0.5 wt% NMO coating layer cannot provide satisfactory electronic conductivity. In addition, its less coverage cannot suppress the formation of thick SEI layer and result in unsatisfactory electrochemical performances. Therefore, we believe that the coating around 1 wt% would be the optimal coating contents of NMO for the NFM cathode material to achieve excellent rate capability.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">O3-type sodium-layered oxides are well-known moisture-sensitive cathode materials. Therefore, O3-type sodium-layered oxides cause structural changes and degradation in cathode performance when exposed to humid air or water.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7,20,58,65</span></sup></a> Water exposure tests were conducted to confirm the effect of the NMO coating layer on moisture stability. In the water exposure test, pristine NFM and 1 wt% NMONFM were compared because 1 wt% NMONFM exhibited the best performance in previous evaluations. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a> shows the initial charge/discharge profiles of the pristine NFM and 1 wt% NMONFM after water exposure. Compared with the initial discharge capacity (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a>), the initial discharge capacity of pristine NFM decreased from 129.48 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> to 91.52 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after water exposure. By contrast, the initial discharge capacity of 1 wt% NMONFM decreased slightly from 127.96 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> to 119.37 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after water exposure. In addition, the initial CE of the 1 wt% NMONFM sample of 85.6% was higher than that of pristine NFM (80.4%). Additionally, to determine the effect of water exposure on the long-term cycle performance, the cycling performance of pristine NFM and 1 wt% NMONFM was evaluated after water exposure. The cycling performances of pristine NFM and 1 wt% NMONFM after water exposure are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7b</a> along with CEs. The discharge capacity of pristine NFM after 100 cycles was 44.79 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, which was significantly lower than that of 1 wt% NMONFM (60.96 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). Consequently, the NMO coating layer improved the moisture stability of the O3-type sodium-layered oxides.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ta06034a-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Initial chargedischarge curves and (b) cycling performance of pristine NFM and 1 wt% NMONFM after water exposure.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To elucidate the cause of the initial discharge capacity loss, poor ICE, and degradation in the cycling performance, XPS analyses were performed on pristine NFM and 1 wt% NMONFM after water exposure. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8ad</a> show the XPS results of the two samples before and after water exposure. The C 1s spectrum consisted of three peaks corresponding to CC, COC, and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>, where the CC peak at 284.9 eV and the COC peak at 286.7 eV correspond to a contamination of the C species and Super P in the NFM electrodes.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> Notably, the Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> peak at 289.1 eV in pristine NFM was stronger than that in 1 wt% NMONFM after water exposure, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8a and c</a>. Similarly, the presence of a peak at 531.3 eV in the O 1s spectrum could be attributed to the residual Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> on the NFM particle surfaces (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8c and d</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit66"><sup><span class="sup_ref">66</span></sup></a> The intensity of the O 1s peak of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> was significantly higher in the pristine NFM sample than in the 1 wt% NMONFM sample, which was consistent with the C 1s results. When NFM was exposed to water and sodium was extracted from the bulk to form NaOH on the surface of the material, Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> formed on the surface of the NFM particles.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit65"><sup><span class="sup_ref">65</span></sup></a> The reaction between NaOH and carbon dioxide in air resulted in the formation of NaHCO<small><sub>3</sub></small>, which was subsequently converted into Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> Therefore, sodium consumption and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> formation were suppressed because the application of the NMO coating layer, which was insensitive to moisture, reduced the exposure of the NFM cathode material to moisture.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig8"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f8_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ta06034a-f8.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d3ta06034a/d3ta06034a-f8.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 8 </b> <span id="fig8"><span class="graphic_title">XPS spectra of (a and b) C 1s and (c and d) O 1s for (a and c) pristine NFM and (b and d) 1 wt% NMONFM after water exposure.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect555"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>This study presented a successful PVP-anchored complex coating process for achieving a uniform nanocoating layer of NMO on an NFM cathode material, resulting in improved electrochemical performance and moisture stability. PVP, with a long-chain structure, served as an intermediate bridge between the NFM cathode material and Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> ions. This enabled the formation of a homogeneous NMO coating on the NFM cathode material surface. XRD, SEM, TEM, and XPS results indicated that the NMO layer was uniformly coated on the surface of the NFM, and a film with a thickness of several nanometers was formed. Comprehensive electrochemical tests were conducted to determine the optimal NMO content for the cathode material. Although the initial discharge capacity was slightly lower for the 1 wt% NMONFM sample, it exhibited significantly improved cycling stability at 1C within the voltage range of 2.04.0 V compared to pristine NFM. The rate capability was also improved by the excellent electrical conductivity of NMO. In addition, the 1 wt% NMONFM sample exhibited better moisture stability than pristine NFM. The initial discharge capacity and cycling performance of the 1 wt% NMONFM sample after water exposure were much better than those of the pristine NFM. The XPS results proved that the formation of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>, which can be formed by exposure to moisture, was suppressed by the NMO coating layer on the NFM surface. Therefore, the NMO coating can effectively solve the chronic problems of NFM and other moisture-sensitive cathode materials. Moreover, the PVP-assisted wet chemical method can also be applied to other cathode materials to obtain uniform coatings, and the coating thickness can be easily controlled by adjusting the amount of coating material precursor and PVP. Therefore, the PVP-assisted wet chemical method used in this study may be a promising strategy for use in various battery applications. Additionally, as part of our future plans, we are committed to design full cell test with hard carbon-based anodes and optimize the physicochemical properties of cathode materials in order to improve and maximize the electrochemical performances of cathodes in this study.</span>

            <br><a href="https://cookiepedia.co.uk/giving-consent-to-cookies" class="privacy-notice-link" rel="noopener" target="_blank" aria-label="More information about your privacy, opens in a new tab">More information</a></div><button id="accept-recommended-btn-handler">Allow all</button><section class="ot-sdk-row ot-cat-grp"><h3 id="ot-category-title"> Manage consent preferences</h3><div class="ot-pli-hdr ot-leg-border-color"><span class="ot-li-title">Consent</span> <span class="ot-li-title">Legitimate interest</span></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0001"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0001" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0001 ot-status-id-C0001"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0001">Strictly necessary cookies</h4></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0001">These cookies are necessary for the website to function and cannot be switched off in our systems. They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work. These cookies do not store any personally identifiable information.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0002"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0002">Analytical cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0002" id="ot-group-id-C0002" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0002"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Analytical cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0002">These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous. If you do not allow these cookies we will not know when you have visited our site, and will not be able to monitor its performance.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0003"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0003">Functional cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0003" id="ot-group-id-C0003" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0003"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Functional cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0003">These cookies enable the website to provide enhanced functionality and personalisation. They may be set by us or by third party providers whose services we have added to our pages. If you do not allow these cookies then some or all of these services may not function properly.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0004"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0004">Targeting cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0004" id="ot-group-id-C0004" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0004"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Targeting cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0004">These cookies may be set through our site by our advertising partners.
